(2021, *Child Care in Practice*, Chiang Mai University) identified acoustic and visual overstimulation as the two most common environmental barriers to learning and participation for preschool children with sensory processing differences. This is consistent with OT clinical practice but is based on a small-scale study in Thailand; cross-cultural validation recommended.

---

## I.6 Lighting — Children-Specific Considerations

**Paediatric Healthcare:** Multiple studies confirm that natural light access reduces anxiety and stress in hospitalised children. Litwin et al. (2023), in an OT-led co-design study at a tertiary-care children's hospital (*HERD* Journal), identified natural light as one of the primary co-designed features. Fricke et al. (2019) confirmed that light, colour, and view to nature are among the few design elements with consistent positive outcome evidence in paediatric settings.

**Education and Childcare:** Children in daylit classrooms demonstrate improved attention and reduced cortisol stress responses compared to artificially lit environments (Hathaway et al., 1992). North-facing or diffuse daylight is preferable to avoid glare.

**Lighting Levels:** For children aged 3 and above, recommended classroom illuminance is 300–500 lux at task surface; healthcare procedure rooms for children may require up to 1000 lux at the procedure area.

**Wayfinding Signage:** Children in CHD/PRE and CHD/INF bands cannot read text; wayfinding must rely on colour, pictogram, floor graphics, and landmark design at child eye-height (approximately 880–1000 mm for ages 3–5). This is addressed in design item CHD-05.

---

## I.7 CHD Design Item Specifications

---

### CHD-01 — Children's Sanitary Provisions: Water Closet

**Population:** CHD/PRE, CHD/ESA, CHD/MCA

**Cross-reference:** Main guidebook items I-03, E-06

**Rationale:** A toilet seat at adult height (430–485 mm AFF) leaves a child's feet entirely unsupported, creates a tipping risk, and prevents independent use of flush controls and toilet paper dispensers. ADA §604.9 is the only confirmed international provision addressing children's WC dimensions; no equivalent mandatory provision exists in BS 8300, DIN 18040, ISO 21542, or other reviewed standards.

**Table I.7.1 — Age-Band WC Specifications**

| Parameter | Ages 3–4 (CHD/PRE) | Ages 5–8 (CHD/ESA) | Ages 9–12 (CHD/MCA) |
|-----------|--------------------|-------------------|---------------------|
| WC seat height (AFF) | 280–305 mm | 305–380 mm | 380–430 mm |
| WC centreline from side wall | 305 mm | 380 mm | 380–455 mm |
| Grab bar (side wall), top of gripping surface AFF | 455–510 mm | 510–635 mm | 635–685 mm |
| Grab bar (rear wall), top of gripping surface AFF | 455–510 mm | 510–635 mm | 635–685 mm |
| Flush control max height AFF | 915 mm | 1015 mm | 1115 mm |
| TP dispenser outlet AFF | 355–485 mm | 355–485 mm | 355–485 mm |

**Technical notes:**

- Where a facility serves a single age group, select all specifications from one column consistently.
- Where a facility serves mixed ages, provide both CHD/PRE and CHD/ESA cubicles in at least one accessible toilet facility per floor.
- Specify an integral footstool at 150 mm height for CHD/PRE to provide foot support — this also improves defecation posture.
- Grab bars at children's heights do NOT satisfy adult grab bar requirements. An accessible toilet facility serving adult carers accompanying children must also meet adult specifications (main guidebook I-03).
- Automatic flush is preferred for all children's cubicles.

**Standards cited:** ADA 2010 §604.9; ADA Final Rule on Children's Facilities (61 FR 37964, 1996).

> **⚠ EVIDENCE NOTE:** No equivalent mandatory provision confirmed in BS 8300:2018, DIN 18040-1/2, ISO 21542:2021, NS 11001-1/2, BCA 2025, CSA B651:2023.

---

### CHD-02 — Children's Handwash Basin

**Population:** CHD/PRE, CHD/ESA, CHD/MCA

**Cross-reference:** Main guidebook sanitary fittings items

**Rationale:** Standard adult lavatory height (800–820 mm AFF rim in most European standards; 865 mm per BS 8300) is inaccessible for independent handwashing by children aged 3–8.

**Table I.7.2 — Children's Basin Specifications by Age Band**

| Parameter | CHD/PRE (3–5) | CHD/ESA (6–8) | CHD/MCA (9–12) |
|-----------|--------------|--------------|----------------|
| Basin rim height AFF | 560–610 mm | 610–680 mm | 680–740 mm |
| Knee clearance below basin | 570 mm depth | 610 mm depth | 685 mm depth |
| Tap/faucet reach from front edge | ≤380 mm | ≤430 mm | ≤480 mm |
| Tap type | Lever or sensor | Lever or sensor | Lever or sensor |
| Tap max operating force | ≤22 N | ≤22 N | ≤22 N |

**Technical notes:**

- Sensor-operated taps are preferred for CHD/PRE and CHD/ESA — removes reach and force requirements entirely.
- Where a fixed-height basin is used, provide a stable non-slip platform step of 150 mm height for CHD/PRE.
- Soap dispenser and towel dispensers must be within child reach range (see CHD-03).
- Mirror: lower edge at ≤1000 mm AFF for CHD/ESA; ≤800 mm for CHD/PRE.

**Standards cited:** ADA 2010 §606.

> **⚠ EVIDENCE NOTE:** No confirmed equivalent mandatory children's lavatory provision in BS 8300, DIN 18040-1, ISO 21542, or BCA 2025.

---

### CHD-03 — Low-Level Operable Parts and Controls

**Population:** CHD/PRE, CHD/ESA, CHD/MCA

**Cross-reference:** Main guidebook controls and hardware items

**Table I.7.3 — Operable Parts Specifications by Age Band**

| Parameter | CHD/PRE (3–5) | CHD/ESA (6–8) | CHD/MCA (9–12) |
|-----------|--------------|--------------|----------------|
| Maximum height of operable elements AFF | 915 mm | 1015 mm | 1115 mm |
| Minimum height of operable elements AFF | 510 mm | 455 mm | 405 mm |
| Door handle height AFF (lever type mandatory) | 700–800 mm | 800–900 mm | 900–1000 mm |
| Light switches, max AFF | 915 mm | 1015 mm | 1100 mm |
| Emergency call pulls (bottom of cord) | ≤100 mm AFF | ≤100 mm AFF | ≤100 mm AFF |

**Technical notes:**

- Door handles in all children's facilities: lever type or push-plate only. No round knobs; no high latches.
- For CHD/INF spaces: all operable elements designed for caregiver use, not child. Exception: emergency or distress call systems must be child-reachable from floor level.
- Maximum door push-force: 22 N for doors used by children (lower than adult 44 N standard).

**Standards cited:** ADA 2010 §309 and Advisory reach ranges.

> **⚠ EVIDENCE NOTE:** No equivalent children's operable parts standard confirmed in BS 8300, DIN 18040-1, or other reviewed jurisdictions.

---

### CHD-04 — Stair and Ramp Handrail — Child Height

**Population:** All CHD bands where stairs or ramps are present

**Cross-reference:** Main guidebook vertical circulation items

**Rationale:** Standard handrail height (865–1000 mm AFF per ISO 21542 and most national standards) is too high for children to grip safely. Children's falls on stairs and ramps are a significant injury risk exacerbated by inaccessible handrail heights.

**Specification:**

- Where a stair or ramp serves children as primary users, a secondary (child-height) handrail is required in addition to the standard adult-height rail.
- Child handrail: 600–750 mm AFF (Sensory Trust UK guidance; consistent with ADA §505 which permits handrail heights between 510–710 mm on playground ramps).
- Both rails must be continuous along the full run, with 300 mm horizontal extension beyond top and bottom.
- Diameter: 25–32 mm (smaller than adult 35–50 mm to suit smaller hand grip).
- Material: smooth continuous surface, no protrusions, rounded returns at each end.
- Dual-rail configuration: adult rail at 865–1000 mm (standard); child rail at 600–750 mm.
- For facilities serving CHD/INF: a third rail at 400–500 mm may be appropriate for toddlers learning to use stairs independently — structural engineering clearance required.

**Standards cited:** ISO 21542:2021 (adult standard, 865–1000 mm); ADA §505; ADA §1002.5 (play areas: 510–710 mm); Sensory Trust UK (mid-rail 600–750 mm).

> **⚠ EVIDENCE NOTE:** No confirmed mandatory dual-rail standard in reviewed national codes.

**Caution:** Dual handrails increase obstruction risk at landings. Ensure landing width accommodates both rails without reducing accessible turning circle.

---

### CHD-05 — Child-Scale Wayfinding

**Population:** CHD/PRE, CHD/ESA

**Cross-reference:** Main guidebook wayfinding items

**Rationale:** Wayfinding systems are designed at adult eye level (\~1500–1700 mm). Children in CHD/PRE have eye levels of 880–970 mm. CHD/ESA eye levels are 1000–1120 mm. Text-based signage is inaccessible to pre-literate children.

**Specifications:**

- Primary wayfinding signage: lower edge at ≤900 mm AFF for CHD/PRE areas; ≤1100 mm for CHD/ESA areas.
- Floor-level wayfinding: colour-coded floor-path systems at ≤50 mm above floor (floor vinyl or inlay); particularly effective in paediatric hospital corridors. Colour must contrast ≥30 LRV with floor surface.
- Pictograms mandatory (not text-only) in all areas serving CHD/PRE. Minimum 100 mm × 100 mm.
- Room identification: combine pictogram and text at child eye level + adult eye level (two-level mounting).
- Landmark design: distinctive visual landmarks (colour accent, sculptural element, mural) at key decision points. Evidence: wayfinding by landmark is more effective than directional signage for populations with limited cognitive mapping capacity (Marquardt, 2011).
- In paediatric hospitals: colour-coding by department/zone is consistently rated positively by children and caregivers (Yu et al., 2024, *HERD Journal*).

**Standards cited:** ADA §703 (general principles); Yu et al. (2024) scoping review; Marquardt (2011) landmark wayfinding evidence.

---

### CHD-06 — Safe Floor Surfaces for Children

**Population:** CHD/INF, CHD/PRE

**Cross-reference:** Main guidebook floor specifications

**Specifications:**

- Play areas (CHD/INF, CHD/PRE): impact-attenuating surface required. ASTM F1292 critical fall height tested material below all elevated play components.
- Indoor floor surfaces (CHD/PRE and above): coefficient of friction ≥0.6 (wet).
- Hard flooring (concrete, ceramic tile) without cushioning: not appropriate as primary floor material in CHD/INF rooms where floor play is primary occupation. Cork, rubber, and quality vinyl are preferred.
- Transition strips between floor materials: maximum 6 mm height.
- Carpeted areas: loop-pile carpet at ≤8 mm pile height. Deeper piles impede wheelchair and walking frame access for children with MOB.

**Standards cited:** ASTM F1292; main guidebook floor specifications.

---

### CHD-07 — Changing Facilities (Infant/Toddler)

**Population:** CHD/INF, and parents/carers of CHD/INF

**Cross-reference:** Main guidebook sanitary provisions

**Rationale:** Nappy-changing facilities are absent from all reviewed international accessibility standards, yet are critical environmental provisions in any building regularly used by families with infants.

**Specifications:**

- Required in any building where CHD/INF populations are a significant user group. Minimum one dedicated changing facility per building; locate on every occupied floor.
- Location: within or immediately adjacent to accessible toilet facility; accessible to wheelchair-using parents and carers.
- Changing table: height-adjustable 450–900 mm AFF to accommodate seated (wheelchair-using) and standing carers.
- Minimum table dimensions: 600 mm wide × 900 mm deep.
- Structural load: 100 kg minimum (table surface).
- Side protection: raised edge ≥75 mm on all sides except approach side.
- Approach space: minimum 900 mm clearance on approach side; 1500 mm turning circle for wheelchair-using carer.
- Waste disposal and nappy bag dispenser: within arm reach of changing table (maximum 1200 mm AFF).
- Handwash basin: immediately adjacent; sensor tap; maximum 1100 mm AFF.

**Standards cited:** BS 8300:2018 Annex D (guidance, not mandatory); ASTM F2285-04 (Standard Consumer Safety Performance Specification for Diaper Changing Tables).

> **⚠ EVIDENCE NOTE:** No confirmed mandatory international standard for baby changing facilities exists in reviewed accessibility codes.

---

### CHD-08 — Drinking Fountain / Water Dispenser

**Population:** CHD/PRE, CHD/ESA, CHD/MCA

**Cross-reference:** Main guidebook items

**Rationale:** Standard drinking fountain spout height (890–1115 mm AFF per ADA; typically 800–1000 mm in European standards) is inaccessible for CHD/PRE children without step assistance.

**Specifications:**

- Children's fountain: spout maximum 760 mm AFF (ADA §602.6 children's exception — parallel approach where spout ≤760 mm).
- Minimum one adult-height fountain must be provided in addition (ADA §602 requires minimum one of each where fountains are provided).
- Alternatively: water dispensers with front-fill or low spout at 600–760 mm AFF; lever or push-button activation at 700–900 mm AFF.
- Operating force: ≤22 N.

**Standards cited:** ADA 2010 §602.

> **⚠ EVIDENCE NOTE:** No equivalent children's fountain provision confirmed in BS 8300, DIN 18040-1, or ISO 21542.

---

### CHD-09 — Furniture and Seating in Children's Spaces

**Population:** CHD/PRE, CHD/ESA, CHD/MCA

**Cross-reference:** Main guidebook seating provisions

**Table I.7.4 — Children's Furniture Specifications**

| Parameter | CHD/PRE | CHD/ESA | CHD/MCA |
|-----------|---------|---------|---------|
| Chair seat height | 280–320 mm | 340–380 mm | 380–430 mm |
| Desk / table surface height | 480–520 mm | 550–600 mm | 600–680 mm |
| Clear knee space under table | 450 mm | 520 mm | 590 mm |
| Waiting area: provide child seating | Yes | Yes | Yes (mix adult/child) |

**Note:** In waiting areas (paediatric hospitals, clinics, schools), provide a mix of child-height and adult-height seating. Adult carers accompanying CHD/INF and CHD/PRE children need to sit at adult height while maintaining close child supervision — provide adjacent, not separated, seating clusters.

**Standards cited:** ADA Advisory §902 (dining and work surfaces — children's dimensions); ISO 9241-5.

---

### CHD-10 — Sensory Environment Design in Children's Settings

**Population:** All CHD bands; specifically NDV children

**Cross-reference:** Main guidebook NDV items; item G-01

**Rationale:** The sensory environment has documented impacts on children's learning, wellbeing, and stress responses. This applies universally, and is intensified for children with sensory processing differences, autism spectrum conditions, anxiety disorders, or post-traumatic stress.

**ASPECTSS Design Index** (Mostafa, 2021, *Frontiers in Psychiatry*):

| ASPECTSS variable | Design principle |
|-------------------|-----------------|
| **A**coustics | Controlled reverberation; acoustic absorbers; quiet zones |
| **S**pace sequencing | Predictable space sequence; clear circulation logic |
| **P**ace escape | Accessible sensory retreat/break-out space within each zone |
| **C**ompartmentalisation | Visual and acoustic subdivision of large spaces |
| **T**ransition | Transition zones between environments (lobbies, decompression corridors) |
| **S**ensory zoning | Low-stimulation vs high-stimulation zones clearly delineated |
| **S**afety | Reduced hazard; predictable spatial boundaries |

**Key minimum provisions for all children's buildings:**

- At least one designated low-stimulation room or alcove per zone.
- Acoustic treatment to ANSI/ASA S12.60 or BB93 in all formal learning spaces.
- Lighting: dimmable; no high-frequency fluorescent flicker.
- Avoid high-contrast clashing patterns in main circulation; reserve visual richness for play zones.

**Standards cited:** Mostafa (2021); ANSI/ASA S12.60-2010; Department for Education UK BB93 (2015); Lersilp et al. (2021).

---

### CHD-11 — Outdoor Play Accessibility

**Population:** CHD/PRE, CHD/ESA, CHD/MCA

**Cross-reference:** Main guidebook recreational provisions

**Key specifications:**

- Accessible route: minimum 1524 mm wide main route through play area; sub-routes 915 mm minimum.
- Surfacing: unitary (poured-in-place rubber, tiles, or play turf) preferred over loose fill for accessible routes. Engineered wood fibre acceptable if maintained to ASTM F1951-99 firmness standard.
- Elevated play component access: ramps preferred over transfer systems (ADA §1008.2).
- Ramp handrail on elevated structures: 510–710 mm AFF (ADA §1002.5).
- Ground-level play components: minimum 50% of all ground-level components on accessible route.
- Transfer platform height: 280–455 mm (ADA §1008.3.2 default 355–455 mm; lower range supports CHD/PRE).
- Shade: required over critical play areas in jurisdictions where UV index regularly exceeds 3.

**Standards cited:** ADA §1008; ASTM F1951-99; AS 4685 (Australia).

---

## I.8 Building Typology Matrices

---

### CHD-BT-01: Paediatric Hospital Wing / Children's Healthcare Facility

**Evidence summary:** Fricke et al. (2019, systematic review) confirmed EBD knowledge for paediatric settings lags behind adult hospitals. Yu et al. (2024, scoping review, *HERD*) identified key preferences: natural light, play spaces, peer social opportunities, low sensory stimulation, accessible routes, and wayfinding legibility. Litwin et al. (2023, OT-led co-design, *HERD*) found child-centred procedure room design reduced anxiety, pain perception, and need for sedation medications.

**Table I.8.1 — CHD-BT-01 Design Item Matrix**

| Item | Primary provision | CHD/INF | CHD/PRE | CHD/ESA | CHD/MCA | CHD/ADO | Design Stage |
|------|-----------------|---------|---------|---------|---------|---------|---|
| CHD-01 | Children's WC | — | ● | ● | ● | ○ | DD |
| CHD-02 | Children's washbasin | ● (carer) | ● | ● | ● | ○ | DD |
| CHD-03 | Low-level controls | ● | ● | ● | ● | ○ | CD |
| CHD-04 | Child-height handrail | ● | ● | ● | ● | ○ | SD |
| CHD-05 | Child-scale wayfinding | ● | ● | ● | ● | ○ | DD |
| CHD-06 | Safe floor surfaces | ● | ● | ● | ○ | — | CD |
| CHD-07 | Infant changing facilities | ● | ○ | — | — | — | SD |
| CHD-08 | Children's drinking fountain | — | ● | ● | ● | ○ | CD |
| CHD-09 | Child-height furniture | ○ | ● | ● | ● | ○ | DD |
| CHD-10 | Sensory environment (quiet zones) | ● | ● | ● | ● | ● | SD |
| CHD-11 | Accessible outdoor play | — | ● | ● | ● | ○ | SD |
● Primary provision | ○ Secondary/recommended | — Not applicable

**Additional requirements:**

- Parent/carer sleeping accommodation: adult-height bed and chair within each paediatric room; clearance for wheelchair-using parent (minimum 900 mm around bed).
- Family-centred care zone: shared family lounge with adult seating + child-scale play area + private consultation space.
- Procedure rooms: natural light; distraction visual displays; dimmable lighting; noise attenuation; emergency call within child reach (CHD-03).
- Ward corridors: colour-coded zones (Yu et al., 2024); floor wayfinding; large-print pictographic room identification.
- NICU/neonatal: low-stimulation lighting; acoustic control per sensory integration evidence — see CHD-10 and main guidebook NEU specifications.

> **⚠ GAP NOTE:** Accessible design for parents with disabilities who have children in paediatric inpatient care is not addressed in the main guidebook or in any reviewed standard. Where parents who use wheelchairs, or who are blind, have children in paediatric inpatient care, their accommodation requires full accessible provision. This is currently unaddressed in all reviewed standards (GAP-022).

---

### CHD-BT-02: Primary and Secondary School

**Table I.8.2 — CHD-BT-02 Design Item Matrix**

| Item | Primary provision | CHD/PRE | CHD/ESA | CHD/MCA | CHD/ADO | Design Stage |
|------|-----------------|---------|---------|---------|---------|---|
| CHD-01 | Age-appropriate WC | ● | ● | ● | ○ | DD |
| CHD-02 | Children's washbasin | ● | ● | ● | ○ | DD |
| CHD-03 | Low-level controls | ● | ● | ○ | — | CD |
| CHD-04 | Dual handrail (stairs) | ● | ● | ● | ○ | SD |
| CHD-05 | Child-scale wayfinding | ● | ● | ○ | — | DD |
| CHD-06 | Safe floor surfaces | ● | ● | ○ | — | CD |
| CHD-08 | Children's drinking fountain | ● | ● | ● | ○ | CD |
| CHD-09 | Age-appropriate furniture | ● | ● | ● | ● | DD |
| CHD-10 | Sensory environment | ● | ● | ● | ● | SD |
| CHD-11 | Accessible outdoor play | ● | ● | ● | ● | SD |

**Acoustic requirement:** All classrooms serving CHD/PRE through CHD/MCA must comply with ANSI/ASA S12.60-2010 (USA) or BB93 (UK) or DIN 18041 (Germany) acoustic performance criteria. Minimum: maximum RT 0.6 s (unoccupied) in rooms ≤250 m³; maximum background noise 35 dB(A).

---

### CHD-BT-03: Childcare Facility (Nursery / Crèche / Preschool)

**Key considerations beyond school standards:**

- All sanitary facilities scaled for CHD/INF and CHD/PRE exclusively.
- Nappy changing facilities (CHD-07) are primary, not secondary provisions.
- Floor-level activities are primary occupation: impact-attenuating floor surfaces throughout.
- No standard adult-scale elements in primary child areas (except adult staff workpoints).
- Sleep/rest area: dedicated room with individual cots; acoustic separation from active play; blackout capability; accessible to wheelchair-using parents.
- Outdoor play: required daily access; accessible outdoor space per CHD-11.

**Evidence:** Lersilp et al. (2021); European Commission ECEC framework quality guidelines (2014).

---

### CHD-BT-04: Indoor Recreational Facility (Sports / Leisure / Swimming)

**Key additional provisions:**

- Swimming pools: steps or ramp pool access (not ladder-only). Handrail at child height (CHD-04) on pool steps. Pool lift for children with mobility impairment (cross-reference MOB items).
- Changing rooms: child-height changing benches (320–380 mm AFF); lockable doors operable at child height; hooks within CHD reach range (CHD-03).
- Sports halls: accessible from main entrance with no steps to court level.
- Spectator seating: sightlines that accommodate seated wheelchair users and children — avoid solid perimeter barriers that block children's sightlines.

---

### CHD-BT-05: Children's Library

**Key provisions:**

- Shelving: accessible to CHD/ESA at maximum reach (1015 mm AFF top shelf). Higher shelves for adult staff use only — clear differentiation required.
- CHD/PRE floor-level seating and reading areas: carpet or cushioned mat area; low-table reading spaces.
- Computer/digital terminals: child-height fixed provision (530–680 mm AFF desk surface for CHD/ESA) or adjustable height.
- Wayfinding: pictographic shelf identifiers at child eye level (CHD-05 standard).
- Quiet zone: designated low-stimulation reading area with acoustic treatment (CHD-10).

---

## I.9 OT Practice Notes: Children's Environments

### Assessment Questions — Children's Facility

1. **Age-band match:** Do the facility's fixed dimensions (WC, basin, furniture, handrails, controls) match the age band(s) of the children primarily served?
2. **Caregiver-child dyad:** Where children require caregiver support (CHD/INF, children with complex disabilities), can the caregiver operate alongside the child without spatial conflict?
3. **Sensory environment:** Does the acoustic, lighting, and visual complexity of the environment suit the sensory profile of the child population? Are sensory retreat spaces available?
4. **Literacy-independent wayfinding:** Can a pre-literate child navigate to the toilet, the play area, and the exit independently?
5. **Outdoor play inclusion:** Can children with mobility impairments access outdoor play on an equivalent basis to ambulant peers?
6. **Transition support:** Are there physical transition zones (decompression lobbies, low-stimulation corridors) between busy and quiet spaces?

### Escalation Threshold — Existing Buildings

Where an existing building used by children fails on items CHD-01 (WC height), CHD-04 (handrail), or CHD-10 (no quiet space), these are P1-level gaps. Children's welfare depends on baseline sanitary independence, fall prevention, and sensory regulation capacity.

Gap Register entry recommended where any CHD-BT-01 building fails to have age-appropriate WC provisions or a sensory quiet area — these represent direct risk to child wellbeing during healthcare encounters.

---

*End of Section I — Section II follows*

## Section II: Accessible Environments for People of Short Stature Including Those with Dwarfism (LPA)

---

## II.0 Purpose, Scope, and Language

This section addresses the design of accessible built environments for people of short stature, including those with dwarfism and related skeletal dysplasias. This population — referred to throughout this section by the code **LPA** (Little People and associated conditions) — is entirely absent from all reviewed international accessibility standards, including ADA 2010, BS 8300:2018, ISO 21542:2021, DIN 18040-1, NS 11001-1, BCA 2025, and CSA B651:2023.

**Language note:** This section uses person-first language — "person with dwarfism," "person of short stature" — consistent with this guidebook's language standards. "Little person" and "little people" are widely used and accepted terms within the community (Little People of America [LPA]; Restricted Growth Association UK [RGA UK]). "Restricted growth" is the preferred clinical and community term in the UK. The term "midget" is considered derogatory and is not used. See also AUCD Language Research Guide (2024).

**Scope:** This section addresses the design of buildings and built environments for adult and adolescent persons of short stature, regardless of the underlying medical condition.

---

## II.1 Why Existing Standards Are Insufficient — The LPA Design Gap

Accessibility standards have developed primarily around two reference populations: the ambulant adult (with and without mobility aids) and the manual wheelchair user. The anthropometric data underpinning most standards reflects a population range from the 5th percentile female (typically \~1520 mm standing height in European populations) to the 95th percentile male (\~1905 mm). People of short stature — adults whose standing height ranges from approximately 1100 to 1470 mm for the most common conditions — fall entirely below the 5th-percentile female design baseline.

The consequences are systematic:

- Controls, counters, ATMs, and service points are positioned above comfortable reach range.
- Reception counters create complete communication barriers — LPA users may not be visible above counter level.
- Standard accessible toilets are designed around wheelchair transfer, not the ambulant short-stature user.
- Stair handrails at standard heights may be unreachable during use.
- Accessible spaces create a second barrier: accessible WC cubicles, designed for wheelchair users, have grab bars at heights inaccessible to LPA users and frequently have raised toilet seats that further complicate use.
- Social and dignity barriers arise in everyday life: being unseen at service counters, needing assistance for routine actions that an average-height person performs independently.

**The "Statuarized Spaces" Problem:** Sociological research conducted with persons with dwarfism in the UK (*Scandinavian Journal of Disability Research*, 2015; *Disability \& Society*, 2020) documented a paradox: people with dwarfism are not only excluded by mainstream spaces but are often also excluded from — and challenged when using — accessible spaces designed for wheelchair users. A "hierarchy of impairments" operates in practice: the accessible WC designed for wheelchair use may have a raised toilet seat that creates new difficulties for an ambulant LPA user. This guidebook takes the position that accessible design must address the full population of disabled persons, including those whose needs differ from the wheelchair-user norm around which most standards have been constructed.

> **⚠ EVIDENCE NOTE:** Peer-reviewed OT literature specifically addressing LPA built environment design is sparse. The most relevant sources are: the *Scandinavian Journal of Disability Research* (2015) qualitative study (UK, n=22, semi-structured interviews); the *Disability \& Society* (2020) paper (UK, photo-elicitation + interviews); and the ANSI accessibility data cited by Kennedy (2003, *Little People*, Rodale Press) regarding reach heights. Design specifications in this section are derived from anthropometric data combined with clinical and community knowledge. Architects should commission post-occupancy evaluations with LPA users.

---

## II.2 Population Sub-Codes: LPA Framework

**Table II.2.1 — LPA Sub-Codes**

| Sub-code | Description | Key design characteristics |
|----------|-------------|---------------------------|
| LPA/ACH | Achondroplasia and disproportionate short stature (most common) | Short limbs, normal-range trunk; arm span significantly shorter than height; macrocephaly possible; may have cervical instability requiring ergonomic head support |
| LPA/PSS | Proportionate short stature (all-over reduced height) | All dimensions proportionately reduced; arm span proportionate to height |
| LPA/MOB | LPA + mobility impairment (wheelchair user) | Requires BOTH LPA AND MOB specifications; wheelchair dimensions will differ from adult standard (shorter seat depth, lower push handle) |
| LPA/JT | LPA + joint complications (SED, pseudoachondroplasia, Morquio) | Pain, joint instability, and mobility limitations in addition to short stature; may require grab bars; lower force thresholds |

**Note on LPA/ACH vs LPA/PSS reach envelopes:** These differ significantly. A person with achondroplasia may have a standing height of \~130 cm but an arm span of only \~110 cm (female) to \~122 cm (male), making comfortable reach substantially shorter than height suggests. A person with proportionate short stature at the same standing height will have a proportionately longer arm span. Design specifications should accommodate the more restrictive envelope (LPA/ACH) wherever both sub-types may be present.

---

## II.3 Anthropometric Data

**Primary source:** Hoover-Fong et al. (2021), CLARITY study — 37,349+ data points from 1,374 subjects with achondroplasia across 4 US centres, birth–75 years. Published in *Orphanet Journal of Rare Diseases*.

**Table II.3.1 — Adult Achondroplasia Anthropometric Summary**

| Parameter | Male (median, SD) | Female (median, SD) | Design use |
|-----------|-------------------|---------------------|-----------|
| Standing height | 129.9 cm (±6.25) | 122.4 cm (±5.9) | Floor-level reach design: 5th percentile female \~110 cm |
| Arm span | \~122 cm | \~110 cm | Controls forward reach: max comfortable \~700–850 mm |
| Leg length | \~50% shorter than general population | \~50% shorter | Step/stair geometry |
| Sitting height | Within low-normal range (spine relatively unaffected) | Within low-normal range | Seating height, desk height |
| Foot length | \~20% shorter | \~20% shorter | Footrest dimensions |

Source: Hoover-Fong et al. (2021) *Orphanet J Rare Diseases*; Merker et al. (2018) *American Journal of Medical Genetics*.

**Australian population data** (Tofts et al., 2017, n=138): Female adult 125 cm (range 116–132 cm); Male 134 cm (range 125–141 cm). Consistent with CLARITY data.

**Key derivation — maximum reach:**

- ANSI survey of LPA members (cited Kennedy, 2003; Stepnwash, 2025): maximum upward reach of persons with dwarfism ≈ 1220 mm (48 inches).
- This finding drove the US reduction of ATM maximum height from 1370 mm to 1220 mm.
- 1220 mm is the maximum, not comfortable, reach. Comfortable forward reach (without extension) for an average LPA/ACH adult female is estimated at approximately 750–900 mm AFF, based on arm span data.
- **Design principle:** 1220 mm is the absolute maximum height for any operable element. Controls in frequently-used or time-pressured contexts (fire escapes, door hardware, payment terminals) should be at or below 1000 mm wherever possible.

> **⚠ EVIDENCE NOTE (GAP-024):** The ANSI reach figure of 1220 mm is cited via Kennedy (2003) as a secondary source. Primary ANSI survey data has not been independently verified for this guidebook. This figure is widely referenced in LPA advocacy and accommodation literature and is treated here as the best available evidence, but primary source verification is recommended before use in mandatory specifications.

---

## II.4 LPA Design Item Specifications

---

### LPA-01 — Reception and Service Counters

**Population:** LPA all sub-codes

**Cross-reference:** Main guidebook counters and service points items

**Problem:** Standard service counters (typically 1050–1100 mm AFF in UK/European practice; 864–915 mm for accessible counters per BS 8300) remain above the sight and reach line of LPA users. Even BS 8300 "accessible counters" at 760 mm AFF — designed for wheelchair users — may be above LPA forward comfortable reach and eye level.

**Specifications:**

- All new public-facing service counters: provide a low-counter section at 760 mm AFF (minimum 760 mm wide) per BS 8300 / ADA wheelchair provision.
- Preferred provision: additional counter section at 660–700 mm AFF (depth 300 mm) where LPA users are a known or likely user group.
- At any counter, a low-level transaction zone must be provided: written material, payment terminals, and communication at ≤760 mm AFF.
- Payment terminals and PINpads: maximum height 1000 mm AFF; screen at ≤760 mm AFF preferred. Screen readable from 300–600 mm distance.
- Glazed screens above counters (security, pharmacy): ensure a sightline opening at 760–900 mm AFF in addition to the standard eye-level opening.
- Intercom/call systems at counters: button at ≤760 mm AFF.

> **⚠ EVIDENCE NOTE:** The sightline barrier at service counters is documented in lived experience literature as creating not only functional barriers but social dignity barriers that constitute a form of discrimination under the UK Equality Act 2010 and the ADA (Van Etten, cited Kennedy, 2003).

**Standards cited:** BS 8300:2018 §9.5; ADA §904.

> **⚠ EVIDENCE NOTE:** No standard specifies additional LPA-specific counter provisions beyond the wheelchair counter provision.

---

### LPA-02 — Door Hardware and Controls

**Population:** LPA all sub-codes

**Cross-reference:** Main guidebook item K-01

**Specifications:**

- Door handles: lever type at 900–1000 mm AFF (standard). Force required must not exceed 22 N for LPA/JT users.
- Internal door openers (button-operated): maximum 1000 mm AFF; minimum 700 mm AFF.
- Where door handles are at standard 1000–1100 mm AFF: provide a secondary pull handle or push plate at 700–800 mm AFF for LPA/ACH users, where reach at 1000 mm requires arm extension and raises safety concerns (cervical instability — see LPA-12).
- Turnstiles: avoided wherever LPA users are anticipated. Turnstile hip height (typically 900–1100 mm) is at or above LPA shoulder height, creating a physical barrier. Gate or pass door must be provided in all cases.
- Smart locks and access systems: control panel at 700–1000 mm AFF; touchscreen accessible at ≤800 mm preferred.

**Standards cited:** Main guidebook K-01; BS 8300:2018 §9.4; ISO 21542:2021 §8.3.

---

### LPA-03 — Lift Controls

**Population:** LPA all sub-codes

**Cross-reference:** Main guidebook vertical circulation items

**Problem:** Controls above 1000 mm require significant overhead reach for an LPA/ACH adult of average height (\~1230 mm standing). Standard lift control panels (900–1500 mm AFF) are theoretically within LPA maximum reach (1220 mm) but require arm extension throughout.

**Specifications:**

- Lift control panel: button range 700–1100 mm AFF.
- Current international standards (ISO 21542 §11.7; BS 8300 §8.6; ADA §407) require button centres at 900–1220 mm for wheelchair accessibility. An LPA-accessible lift must additionally provide controls at ≤1100 mm AFF.
- Best-practice provision: dual-panel lift with second set of controls at 700–1000 mm AFF on the side wall; or control panel spanning 700–1100 mm AFF vertically.
- Emergency controls (door-open button, emergency call, ground floor button): 700–1000 mm AFF.
- Tactile floor designators on buttons: required (ISO 7000; ADA).

**Standards cited:** ISO 21542:2021 §11.7; BS 8300:2018 §8.6; ADA §407.

> **⚠ EVIDENCE NOTE:** No standard specifically addresses LPA lift control placement. The 700–1100 mm range is derived from anthropometric data and ANSI findings cited in Kennedy (2003).

---

### LPA-04 — Accessible Toilet Provisions

**Population:** LPA all sub-codes

**Cross-reference:** Main guidebook item I-03

This is the most complex LPA design challenge. Standard accessible toilet facilities are designed around the wheelchair-transfer model. Several features of wheelchair-accessible toilets create secondary barriers for ambulant LPA users:

| Feature | Impact for LPA users |
|---------|---------------------|
| Raised toilet seat (430–480 mm AFF) | Provides better height than standard (400 mm) — coincidentally helpful for some LPA users |
| Grab bars at 700–800 mm AFF (wheelchair transfer) | At or above comfortable reach for many LPA/ACH adults; unhelpful for balance |
| Large accessible cubicle | Full-length cubicle may feel insecure for LPA users |
| Paper dispensers at 850–1050 mm AFF | May be above comfortable reach |

**Specifications:**

- Toilet seat height: 400–430 mm AFF is the most functional height for ambulant LPA users.
- WC centreline: 460 mm from side wall (standard accessible) — appropriate.
- Grab bars for ambulant LPA users who need balance support: a grab bar at 650–700 mm AFF (wall-mounted, side of WC) provides reachable support for an LPA adult of \~1220–1300 mm standing height. Standard wheelchair grab bars at 700–800 mm AFF are acceptable.
- Flush control: maximum 900 mm AFF. Sensor flush preferred.
- Toilet paper dispenser: 600–750 mm AFF (lower than adult standard of 850–1000 mm).
- Best practice: a "universal" unisex accessible cubicle providing BOTH wheelchair-transfer provisions AND a grab bar at 650–700 mm for ambulant LPA users, plus TP dispenser at 600–750 mm.
- Mirror: lower edge at ≤900 mm AFF.
- Washbasin: 760 mm AFF rim height (wheelchair standard) — coincidentally appropriate for LPA users.
- Soap, paper towel, hand dryer: 600–900 mm AFF.

**Commentary:** People with dwarfism have documented experiences of being challenged or denied access to accessible toilet facilities in the UK because they do not appear to be disabled in the stereotypical (wheelchair-user) sense (*Disability \& Society*, 2020). Universal accessible design must include LPA users. Architects and facilities managers should ensure that accessible WC signage and policy does not exclude LPA users.

> **⚠ EVIDENCE NOTE:** No controlled study confirms optimal WC seat height for LPA.

---

### LPA-05 — Stair and Ramp Handrails

**Population:** LPA all sub-codes

**Cross-reference:** Main guidebook vertical circulation items

**Problem:** Standard handrail height (865–1000 mm AFF per ISO, BS, ADA) is within LPA maximum reach but at or above comfortable grip height for many LPA/ACH adults. A person with achondroplasia ascending a stair may need to reach overhead to grasp a standard-height rail — an awkward and potentially unsafe posture.

**Specifications:**

- All public stairs and ramps: dual handrail recommended.
- Primary rail: 865–1000 mm AFF (adult standard).
- Secondary rail: 600–750 mm AFF, continuous, same diameter and return specification as primary.
- Stair geometry (LPA/ACH): standard tread depth 280 mm and riser height 150–170 mm are appropriate — achondroplasia does not affect leg stride in proportion to stair geometry.
- LPA/JT users (joint complications): lower risers (≤150 mm) and deeper treads (≥300 mm) are recommended. Consistent with the ambulant mobility aid user specification in the main guidebook.
- Note on stair nosings: LPA users with achondroplasia may have shorter foot length (\~20% below average) — standard nosing projections (max 25 mm) are appropriate.

**Standards cited:** ISO 21542:2021 §8.4; BS 8300:2018 §8.5; Sensory Trust UK (mid-rail 600–750 mm).

> **⚠ EVIDENCE NOTE:** No confirmed mandatory dual-rail standard in reviewed national codes specifically addresses LPA users.

---

### LPA-06 — Wayfinding and Signage

**Population:** LPA all sub-codes

**Cross-reference:** Main guidebook wayfinding items

**Adult-appropriate provision:** LPA adults are literate and navigating environments independently. Wayfinding provisions must be adult-appropriate and must not patronise LPA users with pictographic-only signage.

**Specifications:**

- Signage mounting height: lower edge at ≤1000 mm AFF for wayfinding signs in environments where LPA users are a significant population.
- Tactile wayfinding (TWSI): ground-level tactile strips benefit LPA users as well as visually impaired users (ISO 23599:2019).
- Directory/information displays: interactive touchscreens in lobbies and public buildings at 760–1000 mm AFF. Screen tilt preferred over vertical-only display.
- Maps and floor plans: at accessible counter height (760 mm) or as touchscreen at ≤1000 mm AFF.

---

### LPA-07 — Seating and Workstations

**Population:** LPA all sub-codes

**Cross-reference:** Main guidebook seating provisions

**Rationale:** Persons with achondroplasia have sitting height within lower-normal range (trunk relatively unaffected) and may be able to use a standard desk surface if seating height is appropriately adjusted. Leg length is the critical variable — significantly shorter legs mean a standard chair seat height will leave feet entirely unsupported, creating musculoskeletal strain over prolonged use.

**Specifications:**

- Workplace seating: height-adjustable chair (350–550 mm seat height range — lower than standard office chair range of 430–500 mm). Adjustable footrest or integrated footrest required; footrest height 150–250 mm.
- Desk surface: 680–720 mm AFF — appropriate for the seated LPA/ACH adult.
- All height-adjustable standing desks must have a lower-limit seated height of ≤720 mm.
- Public seating (waiting areas, theatres): LPA adults will typically sit in standard seating. Footrests or a low footbar under seating rows is recommended in venues where LPA users are a population group.

> **⚠ EVIDENCE NOTE:** No specific study confirming optimal seating parameters for LPA users was identified for the footrest recommendation; derived from clinical principles.

---

### LPA-08 — Kitchen and Catering

**Population:** LPA all sub-codes

**Cross-reference:** Main guidebook kitchen provisions

**Problem:** Standard kitchen counter heights (860–900 mm European practice; 865 mm BS/ISO) are at or above LPA shoulder height. This makes cooking and food preparation significantly more effortful and potentially hazardous.

**Specifications:**

- Counter height: 750–800 mm AFF preferred (lower limit of wheelchair-accessible counter range).
- Hob/cooktop: maximum 750 mm AFF; touch controls preferred (not physical knobs requiring elbow-height reach).
- Overhead storage: maximum accessible shelf at 1100 mm AFF without step assistance; or pull-down shelf mechanisms.
- Step stool: integral fold-out step at 150–200 mm height in cabinet base units — as supplementary provision for shelf access above 1100 mm.
- Kitchen sinks: 760 mm AFF rim height; knee clearance 685 mm.

**Caution:** Open flames and hot surfaces at counter level create a burn risk for LPA/ACH users whose hands and arms approach at a lower angle than average. Induction hobs reduce this risk. Barrier or front-control design preferred.

---

### LPA-09 — Parking and Approach Routes

**Population:** LPA all sub-codes

**Cross-reference:** Main guidebook site access items

**Context:** LPA users typically drive using standard cars (often with pedal extensions or raised seating) and do not require the wide lateral transfer zone of a wheelchair-accessible bay. However, they do require proximity to the building entrance, as walking longer distances may be tiring for LPA/JT users.

**Accessible parking entitlement:**

- USA: ADA disabled parking — confirmed eligible.
- UK: Blue Badge scheme — confirmed eligible.
- EU, Australia, Canada: recognised under equivalent disability legislation.

**Specifications:**

- No LPA-specific parking bay dimensions required. Standard accessible bay (BS 8300:2018: 2400 mm + 1200 mm transfer zone) is appropriate.
- Parking payment machines and intercom systems: controls at ≤1000 mm AFF. Most existing pay-and-display machines (1200–1600 mm AFF) are inaccessible to LPA users.
- Route from parking to entrance: compact distance (≤50 m recommended for LPA/JT users).

---

### LPA-10 — Swimming Pools and Sports Facilities

**Population:** LPA all sub-codes

**Cross-reference:** Main guidebook recreational provisions

**Specifications:**

- Pool entry: steps or ramp entry (not ladder-only). Handrail at 600–750 mm AFF secondary rail on pool steps — consistent with dual-rail provision recommended at LPA-05.
- Changing rooms: height-adjustable benches preferred. If fixed, provide at 380–420 mm AFF.
- Locker controls: at ≤1000 mm AFF. Lower lockers must be assigned for LPA users; upper sections of standard full-height lockers are inaccessible.
- Turnstiles: not appropriate for LPA users — gate alternative required (see LPA-02).

---

### LPA-11 — Healthcare Settings

**Population:** LPA all sub-codes

**Cross-reference:** Main guidebook healthcare building typology items

**Note:** People with dwarfism use specialist healthcare services at higher rates than the general population for orthopaedic, neurological, and other dwarfism-related care.

**Specifications:**

- Examination tables: height-adjustable to ≤430 mm for patient mounting. Step at 150–200 mm at table end.
- Consultation seating: 380–420 mm seat height; footrest provided.
- Reception counters: per LPA-01.
- Communications design: the physical environment should support direct face-to-face interaction between clinician and LPA patient. Never direct communication to a carer above the LPA patient.

---

### LPA-12 — Cervical and Spinal Considerations (LPA/ACH)

**Population:** LPA/ACH specifically

**Cross-reference:** Main guidebook NEU items

**Clinical note (for OT practitioners):** Achondroplasia involves a narrow foramen magnum at the base of the skull and may cause cervical spine instability or stenosis. Extreme head extension (looking straight up) should be avoided. This has environmental design implications:

- Overhead controls, displays, and wayfinding signs requiring upward gaze should be avoided. Preferred: horizontal sightline (760–1000 mm AFF) or slight upward gaze (≤30° above sightline).
- Overhead screens and departure boards in public transport, airports, and theatres: provide supplementary information at seated/lower eye-level displays.
- This is an OT advisory note, not a mandatory design specification. OT practitioners working with individual LPA/ACH clients should assess cervical status and advise on environmental modifications accordingly.

**Standards cited:** Based on clinical literature on achondroplasia neurosurgical complications (Trotter \& Hall, 2005, American Academy of Pediatrics guidance; Hecht et al. review).

> **⚠ EVIDENCE NOTE:** No specific built environment study identified. Recommendation derived from clinical principles and OT practice.

---

## II.5 Building Typology Matrices

---

### LPA-BT-01: Public Buildings (Retail, Civic, Transport)

**Table II.5.1 — LPA-BT-01 Design Item Matrix**

| Item | Application | Priority | Design Stage |
|------|------------|---------|---|
| LPA-01 | Low-level service counter section | P1 | SD |
| LPA-02 | Door hardware at ≤1000 mm AFF | P1 | CD |
| LPA-03 | Lift controls 700–1100 mm AFF | P1 | DD |
| LPA-04 | Accessible WC with TP at 600–750 mm; flush ≤900 mm | P1 | DD |
| LPA-05 | Dual handrail (600–750 mm secondary) | P2 | SD |
| LPA-06 | Wayfinding signage lower edge ≤1000 mm | P2 | DD |
| LPA-09 | Parking payment machines ≤1000 mm | P1 | CD |

---

### LPA-BT-02: Workplace and Office

**Table II.5.2 — LPA-BT-02 Design Item Matrix**

| Item | Application | Priority | Design Stage |
|------|------------|---------|---|
| LPA-01 | Reception counter low section | P1 | SD |
| LPA-02 | Door hardware and access controls | P1 | CD |
| LPA-03 | Lift controls | P1 | DD |
| LPA-07 | Adjustable seating and footrests at workstations | P1 | DD/SPEC |
| LPA-08 | Kitchen/break-room provisions | P2 | DD |
| LPA-05 | Dual handrail on stairs | P2 | SD |

**HR and facilities note:** Workplace adjustments for a specific employee with dwarfism are a "reasonable adjustment" under the UK Equality Act 2010 Section 20; the ADA (USA); and equivalent legislation in all reviewed jurisdictions. OT workplace assessments should be offered to all employees with dwarfism. Common adjustments include footrests, lower workstation height, adapted seating, step stools at specified locations, and modified storage assignments.

---

### LPA-BT-03: Residential

**Table II.5.3 — LPA-BT-03 Design Item Matrix**

| Item | Application | Priority | Design Stage |
|------|------------|---------|---|
| LPA-04 | WC seat height; TP location | P1 | DD |
| LPA-02 | Door hardware throughout | P1 | CD |
| LPA-08 | Kitchen counter height | P1 | DD/SPEC |
| LPA-07 | Seating and footrests | P1 | SPEC |
| LPA-05 | Stair handrail secondary | P2 | SD |
| LPA-03 | Internal lift controls (where lift provided) | P1 | DD |

**Note on new accessible housing:** Lifetime Homes, CSA/ASC B652:2023, and NS 11001-2 "adaptable housing" provisions do not address LPA-specific modifications. An LPA resident will require: kitchen counter height reduction to 750–800 mm; lower wall-switch heights (all switches ≤1000 mm AFF); and lower storage provision. These are achievable at low additional cost in new construction if flagged at design stage.

---

## II.6 LPA Population Prevalence and Jurisdiction Notes

**Prevalence:** Achondroplasia occurs in approximately 1 in 15,000–25,000 births (Hoover-Fong et al., 2021). Total UK dwarfism population approximately 6,000 (Shakespeare et al., 2007). There are 200+ underlying conditions causing short stature; proportionate short stature is more common but often not classified as a disability.

**Jurisdiction recognition:**

| Jurisdiction | Recognition | Standard gap |
|-------------|-------------|-------------|
| USA | Dwarfism recognised as disability under ADA. Reasonable accommodation required. | ADA dimensions do not specifically address LPA beyond ATM reach height |
| UK | Recognised as disability under Equality Act 2010. RGA UK advocates for explicit recognition. | BS 8300:2018 does not address LPA-specific design |
| EU/Germany | Recognised under SGB IX | DIN standards do not address LPA-specific design |
| Australia | Recognised under Disability Discrimination Act 1992 | BCA 2025 does not address LPA-specific design |
| Canada | Recognised under CHRA and provincial codes | CSA B651:2023 does not address LPA-specific design |
| Japan | Recognised in shougaisha sogo shien legislation | MLIT barrier-free guidelines do not specifically address LPA design |
| Brazil | Lei Brasileira de Inclusão (LBI, 2015) — recognised where functional limitation documented | — |
| Singapore | Recognised under Enabling Masterplan | BCA 2025 does not address LPA-specific design |

**Advocacy organisations:**

- Little People of America (LPA) — USA — www.lpaonline.org (ANSI committee involvement)
- Restricted Growth Association UK (RGA UK) — UK — www.rgauk.org
- Bundesverband Kleinwüchsige Menschen und ihre Familien e.V. (BKM) — Germany
- Stichting Kind en Groei — Netherlands
- Association Grandir — France
- ALPE (Asociación de Personas con Enanismo) — Spain
- Associazione Persone di Bassa Statura (APBS) — Italy ⚠ Requires verification
- Little People of Japan ⚠ Requires verification of current organisation status

---

## II.7 OT Practice Notes: LPA Environments

### Assessment Framework

OT practitioners assessing environments for LPA clients should apply the Person-Environment-Occupation (PEO) model (Law et al., 1996), examining:

1. **Reach envelope:** Which elements in the environment exceed LPA comfortable forward reach (≤900 mm AFF for LPA/ACH) or maximum reach (1220 mm AFF)?
2. **Gaze angle:** Which elements require significant neck extension or upward gaze that may be contraindicated for LPA/ACH clients?
3. **Social visibility:** Where does the physical environment create social barriers (unseen at counters, unreachable signage) that diminish the client's independent occupational performance?
4. **Accessible space paradox:** Do the existing "accessible" provisions (accessible WC, accessible routes) actually accommodate the LPA client's needs, or do they create secondary barriers?
5. **Workplace:** Does the workstation set-up provide appropriate seating height, footrest, and tool reach?

### Priority Modifications in Existing Buildings

| Modification | Priority | Estimated cost tier |
|-------------|---------|-------------------|
| Lower TP dispenser in existing WC | P1 | Low (hardware change) |
| Lower kitchen shelves / add step | P1 | Low–medium |
| Add footrest to workstation | P1 | Very low |
| Add secondary stair handrail | P1 | Medium |
| Lower lift button panel | P2 | Medium–high |
| Modify service counter with low section | P2 | Medium |
| Lower reception counter | P3 | High (structural if counter is fixed) |

### Escalation Threshold

Where an LPA client is denied access to their place of work, their toilet facilities, or their primary means of vertical circulation in a building (lift with inaccessible buttons, no secondary handrail on only stair), these are P1-level items and potential breaches of the applicable equality legislation in all reviewed jurisdictions.

---

*End of Section II — Section III follows*

## Section III: Accessible Environments for Persons of Extreme Height Including Those with Gigantism or Acromegaly (EXH)

---

## III.0 Purpose, Scope, and Language

This section addresses the design of built environments for persons whose height significantly exceeds the population norm. This population is referred to throughout this section by the code **EXH** (Extreme Height).

**Scope:** This section addresses persons whose standing height is approximately 200 cm or greater, or whose build — including arm span, limb length, and body width — creates systematic difficulties in environments designed for the typical adult population range. It includes persons with clinical conditions associated with excess growth hormone (acromegaly in adults; gigantism in those where the condition onset occurred before growth plate closure) as well as persons of extreme height without pathology.

**Language note:** The terms "gigantism" and "acromegaly" are clinical diagnoses and are used in this section in the context of those specific conditions. For persons of extreme height without a diagnosed condition, the design code EXH is used. "Giant" as an unqualified descriptor is considered inappropriate and is not used.

**What this section does not address:** Management of the underlying endocrine conditions (acromegaly, gigantism) is a medical matter outside the scope of this section. This section addresses only the physical built environment.

---

## III.1 Population and Clinical Background

### III.1.1 Conditions and Prevalence

**Acromegaly** is caused by excess secretion of growth hormone (GH) in adults, typically from a pituitary adenoma, after growth plate closure. It causes progressive enlargement of extremities (hands, feet, facial bones) and soft tissue, and increases in height above the person's pre-condition baseline. Prevalence: approximately 40–60 cases per million population (Colao et al., 2019, *Endocrine Reviews*; Katznelson et al., 2014, Endocrine Society clinical practice guidelines). There are approximately 25,000 people with acromegaly in the USA, \~6,000 in the UK.

**Gigantism** (pituitary gigantism) is caused by excess GH secretion in children or adolescents, before growth plate closure. The result is extreme linear height growth during development. Gigantism is substantially rarer than acromegaly — historically documented at approximately 100 cases per century globally (Eugster \& Pescovitz, 1999), though more cases are now diagnosed with improved imaging. Typical adult heights in gigantism range from approximately 200 to 270 cm; cases exceeding 250 cm are exceptionally rare.

**Tall stature without pathology (EXH/TAL):** Persons at the extreme upper end of normal height distribution — above the 99th percentile, typically ≥195 cm for males, ≥185 cm for females in most European/North American populations — may encounter the same built environment difficulties as persons with clinical conditions. Their needs are addressed by the same design provisions.

### III.1.2 Population Sub-Codes

| Sub-code | Description | Key design characteristics |
|----------|-------------|---------------------------|
| EXH/ACR | Acromegaly | Extreme height + disproportionate enlargement of extremities (hands, feet, facial features); joint complications; cardiovascular comorbidities; possible vision impairment (tumour compression of optic chiasm) |
| EXH/GIG | Gigantism | Extreme height (200–270 cm); proportionate or near-proportionate build at the extreme height; joint complications from skeletal loading |
| EXH/TAL | Tall stature without pathology | Extreme height (approximately ≥195 cm male, ≥185 cm female) without endocrine diagnosis; proportionate build |
| EXH/MOB | EXH + mobility impairment | Extreme height + mobility aid use; requires BOTH EXH and MOB specifications |

### III.1.3 Musculoskeletal and Mobility Complications

This is the critical OT dimension of the EXH population. Acromegaly and gigantism are associated with significant musculoskeletal complications:

- **Arthropathy:** 50–70% of persons with acromegaly develop arthropathy, typically affecting the knees, hips, and spine (Wassenaar et al., 2010, *Journal of Clinical Endocrinology \& Metabolism*; Colao et al., 2019). This is caused by both the mechanical loading of an extreme-height skeleton and direct GH-related changes to cartilage.
- **Spinal stenosis and kyphosis:** Vertebral changes are common in acromegaly and gigantism. This may reduce forward flexion and produce a stooped or kyphotic posture in some individuals — meaning that stated standing height may not equal functional clearance height.
- **Carpal tunnel syndrome:** Occurs in 20–50% of persons with acromegaly (Colao et al., 2019). Relevant to door hardware grip, operable part force requirements.
- **Peripheral neuropathy:** May affect hand and foot sensation.
- **Vision impairment:** Pituitary adenoma may compress the optic chiasm, causing bitemporal hemianopia or other visual field loss in some EXH/ACR individuals. Cross-reference VIS population code.

> **⚠ EVIDENCE NOTE:** The clinical complication rates cited above are from acromegaly clinical literature, which is the most evidence-rich source for this population. Gigantism literature is substantially sparser due to extreme rarity. OT literature specifically addressing built environment provision for EXH populations was not identified in searches; recommendations in this section are derived from clinical anthropometric data, ergonomics research on tall workers, and first-principles dimensional analysis. Post-occupancy evaluation with EXH users is strongly recommended.

---

## III.2 Anthropometric Data

### III.2.1 Standing Height and Body Dimensions

For design purposes, the relevant EXH population range is approximately 195–270 cm standing height. The following reference data applies:

**General population 99th percentile heights** (Fryar et al., 2016, CDC/NCHS NHANES data; McDowell et al., 2021):

- Adult males, 99th percentile: approximately 193–196 cm
- Adult females, 99th percentile: approximately 181–183 cm

**Acromegaly height data:** Persons with acromegaly typically exceed their pre-condition height by 2–10 cm, or have heights in the upper 2–3% of the general population if the condition was present for many years before diagnosis (Chanson \& Salenave, 2008, *Orphanet Journal of Rare Diseases*). Heights of 195–215 cm are commonly documented.

**Gigantism height data:** Extremely variable. Heights of 220–250 cm have been documented in the historical and clinical literature. For design purposes, a working maximum of 250 cm is used; specifications should accommodate persons to at least 220 cm standing height as a minimum.

**Arm span:** For EXH/GIG and EXH/TAL, arm span is approximately equal to standing height (standard human proportion). For EXH/ACR, arm span may slightly exceed height due to hand and extremity enlargement.

**Reach envelope:** At a standing height of 210 cm, typical maximum upward reach (fingertip) is approximately 250–260 cm. Comfortable reach (without stretching) is approximately 210–225 cm. This means standard overhead storage at 200 cm, and standard room height at 240 cm, may be below or at the maximum reach of an EXH person — not above it as for the standard population.

**Table III.2.1 — Approximate Anthropometric Parameters for Design Reference**

| Parameter | EXH/TAL 195 cm | EXH 210 cm | EXH 230 cm |
|-----------|---------------|-----------|-----------|
| Standing height | 195 cm | 210 cm | 230 cm |
| Eye level (standing) | \~183 cm | \~197 cm | \~216 cm |
| Maximum upward reach | \~235 cm | \~253 cm | \~277 cm |
| Shoulder height | \~160 cm | \~172 cm | \~189 cm |
| Seated eye height | \~115 cm | \~124 cm | \~136 cm |
| Seated elbow height (AFF) | \~69 cm | \~74 cm | \~82 cm |

Values are approximate, derived from proportional data (Pheasant \& Haslegrave, 2006, *Bodyspace*; DIN 33402-2, 2005 anthropometric data table).

> **⚠ EVIDENCE NOTE:** Direct anthropometric datasets for the EXH clinical population are absent from the published literature reviewed. The values above are derived from standard ergonomic proportional formulae applied to height as a reference variable. Clinical researchers have not established a dedicated anthropometric database for persons with gigantism or acromegaly comparable to the CLARITY dataset for achondroplasia.

---

## III.3 Key Built Environment Barriers

The built environment creates the following systematic barriers for EXH persons, in descending order of frequency and impact:

**1. Doorway headroom:** The dominant UK/European standard door height is 2000–2100 mm (UK BS 8300; EN 14351-1; German DIN). The minimum clear height under a structural lintel is often 2000 mm or less in older buildings. A person of 210 cm standing height has a standing eye level of approximately 197 cm — meaning they are ducking at nearly every door in older buildings, and are at headroom limit at most modern standard doors. Persons above 220 cm tall are typically taller than most standard residential and commercial doorways.

**2. Bed length:** Standard single and double beds (UK, EU) are 190–200 cm internal sleeping length. A person of 210 cm cannot sleep flat in a standard bed. Hospital beds (NHS standard: 210 cm) are at the lower limit; most hotel, residential, and care home beds are shorter.

**3. Ceiling height and overhead hazards:** Standard residential ceiling heights (2400 mm in UK Building Regulations; 2600–2700 mm in Scandinavia) provide nominal clearance for persons to approximately 210–230 cm. However, suspended ceilings, light fittings, signage, and overhead piping frequently reduce effective clearance below nominal ceiling height.

**4. Standard seating and ergonomics:** Standard chair seat heights (430–450 mm AFF) and desk heights (720–740 mm AFF) are designed for a population median of approximately 170 cm male / 163 cm female. At 210 cm height, a person's knees will be above desk level when seated in a standard chair, and their thighs will not clear under standard desk heights.

**5. Vehicle access:** Standard vehicles (including most accessible taxis and minibuses) have cabin headroom of approximately 1200–1400 mm — adequate for the average population but severely restrictive for persons above \~190 cm who need to remain seated, and impossible for persons above \~200 cm when entering/exiting.

**6. Shower and bath:** Standard shower cubicle height (typically 1900–2000 mm spray head and threshold clearance) is below standing head height for persons above 190–200 cm.

**7. Standard accessible WC facilities:** The standard ambulant accessible WC, designed for the mid-height adult, presents unexpected hazards: the overhead projecting cistern in a close-coupled WC may be at face height for an EXH person; grab bars installed at standard height (700–800 mm AFF) may catch against EXH users' lower legs.

---

## III.4 Standards Gap

No reviewed international accessibility standard — including ISO 21542:2021, BS 8300:2018, ADA 2010, DIN 18040-1, BCA 2025, NS 11001-1, or CSA B651:2023 — contains any provision specifically addressing the needs of persons of extreme height.

The standards that do address building headroom (minimum ceiling heights, door heights) do so from a safety perspective (minimum necessary for the general population), not from an accessibility perspective for EXH users.

The gap is therefore total: EXH persons are entirely absent from the international accessibility framework.

> **⚠ EVIDENCE NOTE (GAP-029):** No OT studies specifically addressing built environment design for EXH populations (gigantism, acromegaly, extreme tall stature) were identified in searches of AJOT, OTJR, British Journal of OT, Scandinavian Journal of OT, or allied health databases. This is a complete evidence gap. The recommendations in this section are derived from: clinical literature on acromegaly/gigantism complications (endocrinology journals); ergonomics literature for tall workers (DIN 33402; Pheasant \& Haslegrave); lived experience accounts (advocacy organisations and media interviews); and first-principles dimensional analysis. Gap-register entry GAP-029 is raised for P2 primary research on EXH built environment access.

---

## III.5 EXH Design Item Specifications

---

### EXH-01 — Door and Opening Headroom

**Population:** All EXH sub-codes

**Cross-reference:** Main guidebook entry and circulation items

**Problem:** Standard door heights (2000–2100 mm clear opening in most European and UK standards) are insufficient for persons of 210 cm or above standing height.

**Specifications:**

- New construction: where EXH users are known or likely, clear door opening height minimum 2300 mm. 2400 mm preferred in healthcare, residential care, hotel, and hospitality contexts where EXH users may be guests or residents.
- Door head clearance (clear height from finished floor to underside of structural lintel): minimum 2300 mm in all primary circulation routes.
- Existing buildings: where structural increase of door height is not feasible, identify alternative routes with adequate clearance. Provide wayfinding to EXH-adequate routes from primary entrance.
- Automatic doors: ensure sensor field activates for standing persons to 250 cm height.
- Revolving doors: minimum compartment height 2400 mm to permit upright passage.

**Standards cited:** BS 8300:2018 §8.3 (door minimum 2000 mm clear opening — general standard, not EXH-specific); ISO 21542:2021 §8.2.

> **⚠ EVIDENCE NOTE:** No standard mandates door heights above 2100 mm. The 2300–2400 mm specification is derived from the EXH height range and standard proportional clearance margins (minimum 50–100 mm above standing height of intended user population). No OT study confirms this specification; first-principles derivation.

---

### EXH-02 — Ceiling Height and Overhead Clearance

**Population:** All EXH sub-codes

**Cross-reference:** Main guidebook general provisions

**Specifications:**

- Primary rooms and circulation: minimum 2600 mm ceiling height from finished floor to lowest solid element (including suspended ceiling panels, light fittings, ducting).
- Corridors serving EXH users: minimum 2400 mm clear height throughout (all hanging elements, signs, light fittings above 2400 mm).
- Overhead hazards: all overhead projections below 2300 mm in circulation routes must be clearly signed (tactile and visual warning per main guidebook VIS items) or physically guarded. This is the general requirement for the whole population; the EXH population makes this requirement critical.
- Internal signage: suspend all hanging directional signs from a minimum 2300 mm to underside.
- Structural beams, pipes, and services: where these create obstructions below 2300 mm at head height for EXH users, either raise them or provide high-contrast visual warning at a normal sightline level (1500–1600 mm AFF).

**Standards cited:** UK Building Regulations Approved Document B (minimum habitable room height 2300 mm); BS 8300 general accessibility requirements.

---

### EXH-03 — Bed and Sleeping Provision

**Population:** All EXH sub-codes

**Cross-reference:** Main guidebook healthcare and residential typologies

**Problem:** Standard beds (190–200 cm internal length) are insufficient for sleeping comfort for persons of 210 cm or above.

**Specifications:**

- New construction in residential care, hospital, hotel, and short-stay accommodation: minimum bed provision of one bed per floor with internal sleeping length ≥220 cm.
- NHS standard hospital bed (210 cm): adequate for persons to \~200 cm standing height; specify ≥220 cm internal length for EXH-identified beds.
- Bed frames with adjustable foot end (extending footboard) are preferred in settings where a range of body lengths is expected.
- Bedroom door and en suite door: per EXH-01 (minimum 2300 mm clear opening height) where EXH accommodation is provided.
- Overhead space above bed: minimum 800 mm clearance from pillow level to ceiling or overhead fixture in new construction.

> **⚠ EVIDENCE NOTE:** Standard bed specification data from British Standards (BS 3379) and EN 747-1. No OT study on EXH bed provision was identified.

---

### EXH-04 — Seating and Workstations

**Population:** All EXH sub-codes

**Cross-reference:** Main guidebook seating provisions

**Problem:** Standard seating (430–450 mm seat height; 720–740 mm desk height) creates significant discomfort and musculoskeletal strain for persons of extreme height because knees cannot be accommodated under standard desk heights and legs cannot be positioned flat.

**Specifications:**

- Height-adjustable seating: minimum seat height range 430–600 mm (extending the upper limit of standard adjustable office chairs to accommodate EXH users).
- Desk height: for an EXH/GIG person of 210 cm, comfortable seated desk height is approximately 790–830 mm AFF. All height-adjustable desks in settings where EXH users are employed or regularly use the space must have a maximum height adjustment of ≥850 mm.
- Seated knee clearance under desk: minimum 750 mm height (standard is 685 mm; EXH thigh length requires additional vertical clearance).
- Horizontal leg clearance under desk: minimum 750 mm depth (standard 600 mm) to accommodate proportionally longer lower limbs.
- Public seating: standard public seating (park benches, transport seats, theatre seating) is uniformly too low for comfort at extreme height. Where EXH users are known users of a facility, higher seating options (500–550 mm seat height) should be provided alongside standard seating.

> **⚠ EVIDENCE NOTE:** Specifications derived from Pheasant \& Haslegrave (2006) *Bodyspace* ergonomic proportional formulae and DIN 33402-2 (2005) German anthropometric standard. No specific OT study on EXH workstation design identified.

---

### EXH-05 — Sanitary Provisions

**Population:** All EXH sub-codes

**Cross-reference:** Main guidebook items I-03, sanitary fittings

**Shower:**

- Shower head height: minimum 2100 mm AFF for a person of up to 210 cm standing height; 2400 mm preferred in settings where EXH users are expected. Adjustable-height rail or ceiling-mounted shower head preferred.
- Shower cubicle clear internal height: minimum 2300 mm to any overhead obstruction.
- Shower tray: no unnecessary raised lip — flat entry preferred for EXH/MOB users.

**WC:**

- Standard toilet seat height (400–430 mm AFF) is appropriate for EXH users. Raised toilet seats are contraindicated (already tall; raised seat creates uncomfortable posture, higher centre of gravity).
- Overhead cistern clearance: close-coupled cisterns project to approximately 900–1000 mm AFF from floor — at knee or lower-thigh level for an EXH person. Wall-hung or concealed cistern WCs are strongly preferred in EXH contexts.
- Grab bars at standard height (700–800 mm AFF) may obstruct leg clearance for EXH users; specify with lower edge at 650 mm minimum to allow leg passage.

**Washbasin:**

- Standard basin height (800–820 mm AFF) requires excessive forward stooping for a person of 210 cm — the comfortable basin rim height for an EXH user is approximately 900–950 mm. Where EXH-specific facilities are provided, specify basin rim at 900–950 mm. Where standard basin height is used, note that EXH users will require significant forward bending — grab bars or support rails adjacent to basin are recommended.

---

### EXH-06 — Vehicle Access and Transport Facilities

**Population:** All EXH sub-codes

**Cross-reference:** Main guidebook transport-related items

**Note:** This item addresses built environment provisions around transport (station facilities, vehicle boarding, accessible taxi bays) rather than vehicle specifications, which are outside this guidebook's scope.

**Specifications:**

- Accessible taxi bays: where vehicles have cabin heights <1800 mm, EXH users cannot enter upright. EXH-accessible vehicles (such as wheelchair-accessible minibuses with ≥1800 mm cabin height) should be identified and made available through booking systems where EXH users are a service population.
- Station and platform facilities: seating, wayfinding, and access routes per main EXH provisions. In bus and rail shelters, overhead clearance per EXH-02 (minimum 2300 mm).
- Aircraft boarding: bridge jetties and gate doorways are typically 2000–2200 mm internal height — at the limit or below for EXH/GIG persons. This is largely outside the scope of standard building design; note for facilities management.

---

### EXH-07 — Ergonomics of Controls and Reach

**Population:** All EXH sub-codes

**Counterintuitive challenge:** For EXH persons, the ergonomic problem is not insufficient reach (they can easily reach the standard maximum of 1220 mm) but awkward posture caused by controls, handles, and counters positioned too low. Bending and stooping repeatedly at standard counter heights (760–900 mm) creates significant spinal loading, particularly for EXH/ACR persons who already have arthropathy.

**Specifications:**

- Controls and door handles in EXH-specific spaces: 1000–1100 mm AFF is preferable to the standard 900–1100 mm — in the upper part of the standard accessible range.
- Reception and service counters: standard counter heights (1050–1100 mm) create a bending posture for EXH users. Where EXH-specific service points are provided, a counter section at 1100–1200 mm AFF is appropriate.
- Grab bars and support rails (if provided for EXH/ACR users with joint complications): position at 900–1000 mm AFF rather than the standard 700–800 mm.
- Storage: top shelves at 2000–2200 mm AFF are accessible without stretching for most EXH users; specify accordingly.

---

## III.6 Building Typology Matrix

No dedicated building typology has been developed for EXH, as it is a small population without dedicated building types. The following table summarises item application across the primary building types where EXH users are most likely to be a planned population.

**Table III.6.1 — EXH Design Item Applicability by Building Type**

| Building type | EXH-01 | EXH-02 | EXH-03 | EXH-04 | EXH-05 | EXH-06 | EXH-07 |
|--------------|--------|--------|--------|--------|--------|--------|--------|
| Healthcare — inpatient ward | ● | ● | ● | ● | ● | — | ● |
| Healthcare — outpatient clinic | ● | ● | — | ● | ○ | — | ● |
| Hotel / short-stay accommodation | ● | ● | ● | ● | ● | — | ○ |
| Residential care | ● | ● | ● | ● | ● | — | ● |
| General office / workplace | ● | ● | — | ● | ○ | — | ● |
| Retail / civic / transport | ● | ● | — | ○ | — | ● | ○ |
● Primary provision | ○ Secondary/recommended | — Not applicable

---

## III.7 EXH Population: Jurisdiction and Rights Notes

**Disability recognition:**

- **Acromegaly and gigantism:** Both conditions are recognised as disabilities in most jurisdictions where the condition causes substantial functional limitation (USA ADA, UK Equality Act, EU disability frameworks, Australian DDA). The musculoskeletal complications (arthropathy, neuropathy) are the primary basis for functional limitation assessment. Vision impairment (from pituitary tumour effects) may provide a separate basis for recognition.
- **Tall stature without pathology (EXH/TAL):** Not recognised as a disability in any jurisdiction. Design provisions for EXH/TAL are not legally mandated; they are best-practice provisions for an underserved user group.

**Advocacy and research bodies:**

- Pituitary Foundation (UK) — www.pituitary.org.uk — includes acromegaly patient support
- Pituitary Network Association (PNA) — USA — www.pituitary.org
- European Acromegaly Consortium (EAC) — clinical research network
- EURORDIS (Rare Diseases Europe) — includes gigantism and acromegaly

---

## III.8 OT Practice Notes: EXH Environments

### Key Assessment Priorities

OT practitioners working with EXH clients should assess:

1. **Headroom audit:** Does the person's primary living, working, and transit environment provide adequate headroom throughout? Identify all overhead hazards below 2300 mm in primary circulation routes.
2. **Sleeping adequacy:** Is the bed of adequate length? Poor sleep posture from insufficient bed length is a significant long-term musculoskeletal risk.
3. **Workstation ergonomics:** Is the work surface height adequate to prevent persistent spinal flexion? Footrest is not required for EXH users (feet are flat and well-positioned); the priority is elevated desk height.
4. **Joint protection (EXH/ACR):** For persons with acromegaly-related arthropathy, prioritise reductions in bending and stooping at standard-height counters, controls, and basins. This is a direct OT domain.
5. **Handgrip:** For persons with acromegaly-related carpal tunnel syndrome or peripheral neuropathy, assess door hardware and operable parts — lever handles with low operating force preferred.
6. **Visual field (EXH/ACR with visual field loss):** Cross-reference main guidebook VIS provisions as appropriate.

### Priority Modifications in Existing Buildings

| Modification | Priority | Estimated cost tier |
|-------------|---------|-------------------|
| Adjustable-height desk | P1 | Low–medium |
| Extended/adjustable bed (residential and healthcare) | P1 | Low (equipment change) |
| Adjustable-height shower head | P1 | Low |
| Remove low-clearance overhead hazards in circulation routes | P1 | Low (typically removal or repositioning) |
| Raise door head clearance | P2–P3 | High (structural) |
| Concealed cistern WC replacement | P2 | Medium |

---

*End of Section III — Section IV follows*

## Section IV: Accessible Environments for Persons of Large Body Size (BAR)

---

## IV.0 Purpose, Scope, and Language

This section addresses the design of built environments for persons of large body size — persons whose weight, body width, or physical dimensions exceed the standard design parameters used in most building specifications.

**Large body size is not a disability.** This section is presented as a dedicated chapter rather than as a disability code because large body size is not an impairment, limitation, or disability under any reviewed legal framework.
